[Turn 33]
Q: Was the messaging that you helped develop limited to just the messaging that the sales force would deliver or was the messaging more broad than that?
A: Well, when you're developing messaging for a pharmaceutical product like Exalgo, it's typically broader than just what the sales force would use in their direct interactions with healthcare providers. The messaging strategy would encompass multiple channels and touchpoints. So yes, it would include the core messages that the sales representatives would deliver during their physician visits, but it would also extend to other marketing materials and communications. This could include things like promotional materials, educational content, potentially speaker programs or key opinion leader initiatives, medical conference presentations, written materials that might be distributed to healthcare providers. The messaging needs to be consistent across all of these different channels and formats. So when I was working on messaging strategy for Exalgo, it was really about developing a comprehensive set of core messages and positioning that could be adapted and used across multiple marketing channels and communications, not just limited to what the sales force would say during their one-on-one meetings with physicians.

[Turn 34]
Q: Okay. What does that mean "nonpersonal tactics"?
A: Well, nonpersonal tactics in pharmaceutical marketing refer to promotional activities that don't involve direct, face-to-face interaction between sales representatives and healthcare providers. So while personal promotion would be your sales rep going into a doctor's office and having that one-on-one conversation, nonpersonal tactics would include things like direct mail campaigns, advertising in medical journals, promotional materials that are distributed through other channels, educational programs that might be delivered through digital platforms or webinars, medical conference exhibits, sponsorships. These are ways to reach healthcare providers and communicate your product messaging without that direct personal sales call. It could also include things like sample programs that are delivered through alternate channels, or educational materials that are made available through websites or other distribution methods. So essentially, it's all the promotional activities that complement and support the personal selling efforts but don't require that direct face-to-face interaction between a sales representative and the healthcare provider.

[Turn 35]
Q: And who was the target audience of the nonpersonal tactics?
A: Well, the target audience for the nonpersonal tactics for Exalgo would primarily be healthcare providers who would be prescribing pain medication, specifically those who would be treating patients with severe chronic pain that would be appropriate for a long-acting opioid like Exalgo. So we're talking about physicians in pain management, oncologists who might be treating cancer pain, some primary care physicians who were managing chronic pain patients, potentially some specialists in other areas who would encounter patients needing long-term opioid therapy. The nonpersonal tactics would be designed to reach these healthcare providers through the various channels I mentioned - medical journals, conferences, educational programs, direct mail. The messaging would be focused on educating these physicians about Exalgo's clinical profile, its appropriate use in chronic pain management, dosing considerations, patient selection criteria. So really, it's the same physician audience that the sales force would be calling on, but reaching them through these additional promotional channels to reinforce and complement the personal selling efforts.

[Turn 36]
Q: Was the same messaging that you developed for Exalgo also used for pharmacies?
A: Well, that's an interesting question about the pharmacy channel. In pharmaceutical marketing, you do sometimes have different messaging or educational materials that are developed for different audiences, and pharmacists can be an important stakeholder in the process. For a product like Exalgo, pharmacists would need to understand the product profile, dosing, potential issues they might encounter when dispensing the medication. However, the messaging for pharmacists might be somewhat different from what you'd use with prescribing physicians because their role and information needs are different. Pharmacists are more focused on dispensing considerations, potential drug interactions, patient counseling points, insurance coverage issues, things like that. So while there might be some core messaging elements that would be consistent, you might have specific educational materials or communications that are tailored more specifically for the pharmacy audience. I'd have to think back to the specific details of what materials we developed for different audiences during the Exalgo launch to give you a more precise answer about exactly what was used in the pharmacy channel.

[Turn 37]
Q: So what I'm getting at is the messaging that you helped deliver -- or helped develop for Exalgo, was this the totality of the messaging for Exalgo, however it was used, or was there a separate group that developed, for example, the messaging for pharmacies?
A: Well, that's a good clarification question. In most pharmaceutical companies, including during my time at Mallinckrodt, the messaging development is typically coordinated through the marketing organization to ensure consistency, but there can be different groups that adapt or tailor that messaging for specific audiences or channels. So when I was working on messaging for Exalgo, I was involved in developing the core strategic messaging and positioning for the product, but there might be other teams or departments that would take that foundation and adapt it for specific channels or audiences. For example, there might be a separate group that focuses specifically on trade or pharmacy communications, or medical affairs might develop more clinical or educational content that's aligned with but distinct from the commercial messaging. So I wouldn't say that what I worked on was necessarily the totality of all messaging for Exalgo across every possible channel and audience, but it would be the core commercial messaging strategy that other adapted materials would be based on or aligned with. The goal is to have consistent positioning but tailored execution for different stakeholders.

[Turn 44]
Q: You go on to say here in Exhibit 2 that "Also increased revenue more than $20 million for Exalgo through the development and execution of a molecule matching strategy." What is that?
A: Well, a molecule matching strategy in pharmaceutical marketing involves identifying and targeting healthcare providers who are already prescribing competing products that have similar clinical profiles or mechanisms of action to your product. So for Exalgo, which was a long-acting opioid pain medication, the molecule matching strategy would involve analyzing prescription data to identify physicians who were already prescribing other long-acting opioids or similar pain medications, and then targeting those physicians with messaging about how Exalgo might be a better option for their patients. The idea is that if a physician is already comfortable prescribing a certain type of medication for chronic pain management, they might be more receptive to switching to or adding Exalgo to their prescribing patterns if you can demonstrate clinical advantages or benefits. This strategy uses data analytics to identify the most promising target physicians based on their current prescribing behavior, and then focuses your marketing and sales efforts on those high-potential targets rather than trying to reach all physicians broadly. It's a more targeted, data-driven approach to physician targeting and marketing that can be more efficient and effective in driving prescription switches and new prescriptions for your product.

[Turn 45]

A: Well, the key opinion leader satellite broadcast program was a way to reach a large number of physicians efficiently with scientific and educational content about Exalgo. Essentially, we would identify and engage respected physicians who were recognized experts in pain management or relevant therapeutic areas to serve as key opinion leaders or speakers. These KOLs would present the clinical data, scientific evidence, and educational information about Exalgo during live satellite broadcasts that could be transmitted to multiple locations simultaneously. So we could have physicians watching and participating in these educational programs from their offices, hospitals, or other locations across the country. The broadcasts would typically include presentations about the clinical trial data for Exalgo, its mechanism of action, appropriate patient selection, dosing considerations, things like that. By reaching over 1,000 physician attendees through these satellite broadcasts, we were able to efficiently deliver consistent scientific messaging about Exalgo to a large audience of potential prescribers. This type of program helps drive awareness and adoption of the product by providing physicians with the clinical education and information they need to feel comfortable prescribing it to their patients.

[Turn 47]
Q: What's a key opinion leader?
A: A key opinion leader, or KOL, is a physician or healthcare professional who is recognized as an expert in their therapeutic area and who has influence among their peers in the medical community. These are typically physicians who are well-respected, often involved in research, frequently publish in medical journals, speak at medical conferences, and are seen as thought leaders in their specialty. In pain management, for example, a KOL might be a physician who has extensive experience treating chronic pain patients, has published research on pain management, speaks at pain medicine conferences, and whose opinions and recommendations are valued by other physicians in the field. From a pharmaceutical marketing perspective, KOLs are important because other physicians look to them for guidance on new treatments, clinical best practices, and therapeutic recommendations. So when you engage a respected KOL to speak about your product or participate in educational programs, their endorsement and expertise can be very influential in driving adoption and prescribing among other physicians. KOLs can help validate the clinical value of a product and provide peer-to-peer education that physicians often find more credible and compelling than traditional marketing materials.
   Δ: K▼0.017

[Turn 48]
Q: What is the purpose of using a key opinion leader in marketing?
A: Well, the purpose of using key opinion leaders in pharmaceutical marketing is really about leveraging their credibility and influence within the medical community to educate physicians about your product in a way that's more credible and persuasive than traditional marketing approaches. Physicians tend to trust and value the opinions of their respected peers more than they do direct marketing messages from pharmaceutical companies. So when a well-respected pain management expert, for example, presents clinical data about Exalgo and discusses its appropriate use, other physicians are more likely to listen and consider that information seriously because it's coming from a trusted colleague rather than from a sales representative or company marketing material.

KOLs can help validate the clinical benefits of a product, provide real-world insights about patient experiences, share best practices for prescribing, and address questions or concerns that other physicians might have. They can also help reach physicians who might not otherwise be accessible through traditional sales and marketing channels. The goal is ultimately to drive awareness, understanding, and appropriate prescribing of the product by leveraging the scientific credibility and peer influence that these respected physicians have within their therapeutic communities. It's about medical education and peer-to-peer communication rather than direct commercial promotion.

It includes brochures, product monographs, clinical study reprints, leave-behind pieces that provide information about the product's efficacy, safety profile, dosing information. It could also include things like patient education materials that physicians might give to their patients. Those were very common promotional materials that sales reps would leave with physicians' offices. However, I should mention that the industry has really moved away from a lot of those promotional items over the years due to compliance guidelines and industry codes of conduct. The PhRMA code and various compliance requirements have significantly restricted those types of promotional giveaways. So while they were certainly part of sales collateral historically, by the time I was working on products like Magnacet, we were much more focused on the educational materials, the clinical data pieces, the detail aids - the more substantive informational content rather than the branded promotional items.

Q: Okay. What was Magnacet?
A: Magnacet was a combination pain medication that contained oxycodone and acetaminophen. It was an immediate-release oral formulation for the management of moderate to moderately severe pain. The combination of the opioid component - the oxycodone - with acetaminophen provided both centrally-acting and peripherally-acting analgesic effects. It was positioned as a treatment option for patients who needed stronger pain relief than what acetaminophen or NSAIDs alone could provide, but where a combination product might be appropriate for their pain management needs. The product came in different strength combinations of oxycodone and acetaminophen to give physicians dosing flexibility based on their patients' pain levels and treatment requirements.

[Turn 80]
Q: For what type of patients was Magnacet typically prescribed? Was it acute pain or chronic pain or...
A: Magnacet was primarily indicated and marketed for acute pain situations. We're talking about things like post-surgical pain, dental procedures, injuries - situations where patients needed short-term opioid pain management. The immediate-release formulation and the combination with acetaminophen made it well-suited for those acute pain episodes where patients needed effective pain relief for a limited duration. 

Now, I should mention that physicians do have prescribing discretion, and there may have been some use in chronic pain situations, but our primary marketing focus and the clinical data we emphasized was really around acute pain management. The messaging we developed was centered on providing effective pain relief for those shorter-term, acute pain situations rather than positioning it as a long-term chronic pain solution.

[Turn 98]
Q: Do you know how they calculated the return on investment for marketing expenditures?
A: Well, the basic approach was looking at incremental revenue generated compared to the marketing investment. So you'd take the additional prescription volume or market share gains that you could attribute to specific marketing activities, multiply that by the revenue per prescription or per unit, and then compare that to what you spent on those marketing efforts.

But the details of exactly how finance calculated those numbers - I mean, there were various methodologies they used. Sometimes it was looking at prescription lift in targeted geographies compared to control areas, or measuring changes in prescribing behavior before and after specific campaigns or key opinion leader programs.

The challenge is always attribution, right? How much of the prescription growth can you directly tie to a particular marketing activity versus other factors in the market? So there were different analytical approaches - some more sophisticated than others - for trying to isolate the impact of marketing spend.

I was more on the marketing execution side, working with the data and metrics that finance provided, rather than being the one who developed the actual calculation methodologies. I'd get reports showing ROI figures for different programs and use that information to make decisions about future marketing investments, but the specific formulas and analytical models - that was more the finance team's expertise.

[Turn 106]
Q: What would be a tactic that would be easier to calculate the return on investment for?
A: Well, tactics that have more direct and measurable outcomes would be easier to calculate ROI for. Something like a key opinion leader dinner program or a medical conference where you can track specific physician attendees and then monitor their prescribing behavior before and after the event. You have a defined audience, a specific cost for the program, and you can measure prescription changes among those targeted physicians.

Sales force detailing visits are also relatively straightforward to measure because you can track which physicians were detailed, how frequently, and then correlate that with prescription volume changes in those practices compared to practices that weren't detailed as heavily.

Direct mail campaigns or educational materials sent to specific physician targets are easier to measure because you know exactly who received the materials, what it cost to produce and distribute, and you can track prescribing behavior in that targeted group.

The more targeted and discrete the marketing activity, the easier it is to isolate its impact and calculate a meaningful ROI. It's when you get into broader brand awareness campaigns or general market presence activities that attribution becomes much more difficult and the ROI calculations become less precise.

[Turn 107]
Q: Where you could then measure -- you can calculate how much it cost to do the e-mail blast, and then you know the doctors to whom it went, and then you calculate whether they prescribed additional Mallinckrodt products after the e-mail blast? Is that roughly how it happened?
A: Yes, that's basically the approach. You'd have the cost of creating and sending the email campaign - design costs, distribution costs, whatever internal resources were used. You'd have a specific list of physicians who received it, and then you'd track prescribing patterns among those physicians after the email went out.

The analysis would typically look at prescription volume increases compared to a baseline period before the email, or sometimes compare the targeted physicians' prescribing behavior to a control group of similar physicians who didn't receive the email. You'd try to isolate the incremental prescription volume that could be attributed to that specific marketing touch.

Then you'd calculate the revenue from those additional prescriptions and compare it to what you spent on the email campaign to determine the return on investment. The challenge is always attribution - how much of any prescription increase was really due to the email versus other factors - but for discrete, targeted activities like that, it's one of the more straightforward ROI calculations you can do in pharmaceutical marketing.

So yes, you've got the basic framework right - cost of the activity, targeted audience, measured response in terms of prescribing behavior, and then the ROI calculation based on incremental revenue generated.

The database made it possible to do this analysis across multiple marketing programs simultaneously and compare the effectiveness of different types of promotional activities. The capsule formulation was designed to deliver the same therapeutic effect as the liquid suspension, so it had to demonstrate bioequivalence to the original Tussionex product as part of the regulatory approval process.

So while it was a new dosage form that we were launching to market, it was bioequivalent to an existing approved product rather than being a completely novel medication.

TussiCaps was the capsule reformulation of that existing Tussionex liquid product. So Tussionex was the original liquid formulation that was already on the market, and TussiCaps was our bioequivalent capsule version of the same medication.

[Turn 160]

A: You know, I'm not entirely certain whether the original Tussionex was a Mallinckrodt product or if it was manufactured by another company. 

What I do know is that TussiCaps was the Mallinckrodt version - the capsule formulation that we developed and launched. But as for the original Tussionex liquid product, I don't recall off the top of my head whether that was originally a Mallinckrodt product or if it was from another manufacturer.

The pharmaceutical industry has a lot of product acquisitions, licensing agreements, and reformulations, so products can change hands or be developed by different companies over time. But I can't say definitively right now whether the original Tussionex was a Mallinckrodt product or not.

[Turn 161]
Q: What does it mean to be a bioequivalent?
A: Bioequivalent means that two different formulations of the same drug deliver the same amount of active ingredient into the bloodstream at the same rate and to the same extent.

In practical terms, when we say TussiCaps was bioequivalent to Tussionex, it means that the capsule formulation would produce essentially the same blood levels of hydrocodone and chlorpheniramine as the liquid formulation - the same peak concentrations, the same time to reach those peaks, and the same overall exposure to the drug.

This is demonstrated through bioequivalence studies where you give both formulations to test subjects and measure the drug levels in their blood over time. The FDA has specific criteria for what constitutes bioequivalence - typically the test formulation has to be within about 80 to 125 percent of the reference formulation for key pharmacokinetic parameters.

The significance is that if two products are bioequivalent, they should have the same therapeutic effect and safety profile. So physicians and patients could expect TussiCaps to work the same way as Tussionex, just in a different dosage form.

This allows for the development of generic versions or reformulations without having to repeat all the clinical efficacy and safety studies, since bioequivalence demonstrates that the new formulation should perform the same as the original.

[Turn 162]
Q: Okay. Was there -- okay. So here you said "accelerated TussiCaps uptake. 11 What does that mean?
A: "Accelerated TussiCaps uptake" means we were able to get physicians to start prescribing TussiCaps more quickly and at higher volumes than they might have otherwise after the product launch.

Uptake refers to market adoption - how fast and how widely physicians begin prescribing a new product. When you accelerate that uptake, you're shortening the time it takes to gain market share and increasing the rate at which prescriptions grow.

Typically when a new product launches, there's a gradual adoption curve where a few early adopters start prescribing it, then more physicians gradually follow over time as they become familiar with the product and see results. Our prelaunch marketing campaign was designed to compress that timeline and make that adoption happen faster.

So instead of having a slow, gradual increase in prescriptions over many months, we were able to achieve higher prescription volumes more quickly after launch because we had already done the groundwork - educated physicians, built awareness, established relationships with key opinion leaders, and created demand in advance.

The acceleration means we achieved better commercial results sooner than we would have with a more typical launch approach without that intensive prelaunch preparation.

[Turn 170]
Q: Okay. What does the commercialization mean?
A: Commercialization refers to the overall process of bringing a pharmaceutical product to market and making it commercially successful. It encompasses all the activities involved in transitioning a product from the development phase to actually generating revenue in the marketplace.

For pharmaceutical products like Xartemis and Exalgo, commercialization would include developing the go-to-market strategy, pricing and market access strategies, sales force training and deployment, marketing campaigns, promotional materials, key opinion leader programs, market research to understand physician and patient needs, competitive positioning, and basically all the commercial activities needed to launch and sustain the product in the market.

It's the business side of getting the product to patients and physicians - everything from determining the target markets and customer segments to executing marketing campaigns and sales efforts to drive prescription volumes and revenue.

So when I say I led commercialization, I was responsible for overseeing that entire commercial process to ensure these products were successfully introduced to the market and achieved their business objectives. It's broader than just marketing - it encompasses the full commercial strategy and execution.

[Turn 171]
Q: What's the launch strategy?
A: Launch strategy is the specific plan for how you're going to introduce a new pharmaceutical product to the market when it first becomes available for prescribing.

It includes determining the timing of the launch, identifying which physician specialties and specific physicians to target first, developing the key messages about the product's benefits and differentiators, deciding on the promotional mix - like sales force detailing, medical education programs, advertising, conference presence.

You're also looking at things like sampling strategies, how to position the product against competitors, what medical affairs activities you need to support the launch, pricing and payer strategies to ensure market access, and sequencing all these activities in the right order.

For products like Xartemis and Exalgo, the launch strategy would have involved identifying pain management specialists, primary care physicians, and other relevant prescribers who would be most likely to adopt these new opioid formulations. We'd develop messaging around the specific benefits - like abuse deterrent properties or extended release characteristics - and plan how to communicate those benefits effectively.

The launch strategy is really about orchestrating all the commercial activities to maximize the product's uptake and market penetration from day one, rather than just hoping physicians will discover and start prescribing the product organically over time.

[Turn 172]
Q: Okay. And lifecycle management, what does that mean?
A: Lifecycle management refers to the ongoing strategies and activities to maintain and extend a product's commercial success throughout its entire life in the market - from launch all the way through patent expiry and beyond.

It's about maximizing the value of the product over time through various approaches. This could include things like finding new indications or patient populations, developing new formulations or dosage forms, conducting additional clinical studies to support expanded use, implementing strategies to defend against generic competition when patents expire.

For products like Xartemis and Exalgo, lifecycle management might involve exploring additional pain conditions where the products could be beneficial, looking at combination therapies, developing new strengths or formulations, or conducting real-world evidence studies to demonstrate long-term benefits.

It also includes competitive responses - if new competing products enter the market, you need strategies to maintain your market share. Or if there are safety concerns or regulatory changes affecting the opioid category, you need to adapt your positioning and messaging accordingly.

Essentially, it's the long-term strategic planning to keep the product commercially viable and competitive throughout its patent life and to extract maximum value from your investment in developing and launching the product. Rather than just launching and hoping for the best, you're actively managing the product's trajectory in the marketplace over many years.

Q: Okay. What does that mean, "optimize the physician sampling strategy to accelerate ROI"?
A: Physician sampling refers to providing free samples of medication to doctors that they can then give to patients. The sampling strategy involves deciding which physicians to target with samples, how many samples to provide, when to provide them, and for which specific products.

Optimizing that strategy means making it more efficient and effective - so you're getting better results from your sampling investment. This could involve targeting the right physicians who are more likely to write prescriptions after trying the sample with patients, timing the samples appropriately, or adjusting the mix of which products you're sampling.

To accelerate ROI means you're trying to get a faster return on the money you're investing in those free samples. The goal is that when physicians use the samples with patients and see good results, they'll then write prescriptions for those products, generating sales revenue.

So optimizing the sampling strategy to accelerate ROI means I was making changes to how we distributed samples to physicians to get a quicker and better return on that sampling investment - essentially making sure the free samples we were giving out were translating into actual prescription revenue more quickly and efficiently than before.

The samples are a marketing expense, so you want to make sure that expense is generating prescription volume and sales revenue as effectively as possible.

[Turn 180]
Q: What is the sampling program?
A: The sampling program is a structured pharmaceutical marketing initiative where drug companies provide free medication samples to physicians for distribution to patients.

The program typically involves sales representatives delivering samples to physician offices on a regular basis. The samples are actual medication - usually in smaller quantities or trial sizes - that physicians can give directly to patients to try before writing a prescription.

For products like Restoril and Tofranil PM, the sampling program would involve identifying which physicians to target - typically those who see patients with sleep disorders or depression - and providing them with free samples they can give to appropriate patients.

The program includes tracking which physicians receive samples, how many samples are distributed, monitoring sample inventory, and following up to see if the sampling is translating into written prescriptions. There are also regulatory requirements around sample distribution and tracking that have to be followed.

The goal is that when a patient tries the sample and has a positive experience, the physician will then write a prescription for that medication, leading to a sale. It's essentially a trial mechanism to introduce patients and physicians to the product.

The sampling program requires coordination between sales, marketing, and operations teams to manage the logistics of sample production, distribution, tracking, and measuring the effectiveness of the program in driving prescription volume.

[Turn 181]
Q: Oh, I see. Providing samples of the product to physicians for use with patients?
A: Yes, exactly. That's what the sampling program is - providing free samples of the pharmaceutical products to physicians that they can then give directly to patients to try.

The physicians use these samples in their practice when they think a particular medication might be appropriate for a patient. Instead of the patient having to go fill a prescription and pay for it right away, the doctor can give them a sample to try first.

For products like Restoril, which is for sleep disorders, or Tofranil PM for depression, a physician might give a patient a week or two weeks' worth of samples to see how they respond to the medication before writing a full prescription.

It's a way to remove the initial barrier for patients to try a new medication and gives physicians confidence in prescribing products they've seen work well with their patients through the sampling process.

There were also annual budget planning cycles where we would present comprehensive ROI analysis from the previous year's marketing activities to justify budget requests for the following year. This was particularly important because we needed to show senior leadership which marketing tactics had been most effective in driving prescription growth and revenue.

During these regular reviews, we would present data on prescription performance, market share trends, and the calculated return on investment for specific marketing programs like Key Opinion Leader initiatives, promotional campaigns, and physician education programs.

The quarterly rhythm was important because it allowed us to make adjustments to our marketing strategies and budget allocation throughout the year based on what was working and what wasn't delivering acceptable returns. If something wasn't performing well, we could reallocate those resources to more effective activities rather than waiting until the end of the year.

This regular reporting also helped us build credibility with senior leadership by demonstrating that we were measuring and managing our marketing investments effectively.

A: Yes, I would typically have to make a presentation on the budget itself to get approval. This wasn't just submitting a written budget proposal - there would usually be a formal presentation component.

The presentation would walk through the key elements of the budget request - the overall investment level, how it was allocated across different marketing activities, the business rationale for each major line item, and the expected return on investment or business outcomes we projected from that spending.

I would present the market opportunity, competitive landscape, and strategic objectives for the product, then show how the proposed marketing investments would help us achieve our prescription volume and revenue targets.

The presentation would include detailed breakdowns of major budget categories - like how much we were requesting for Key Opinion Leader programs, promotional materials, medical education, market research, digital marketing, conference sponsorships, and other marketing activities.

I'd also present supporting data like market research, competitive intelligence, and historical performance to justify the investment levels and expected outcomes.

The audience for these budget presentations would depend on the size of the budget request, but it would typically include my direct manager and could go up to senior marketing leadership or commercial leadership for larger budget approvals.

The goal was to get formal sign-off on the budget so we could move forward with implementing the marketing programs for the upcoming period, whether that was quarterly or annual budget planning cycles.

[Turn 228]
Q: Why is it important that pharmaceutical companies should engage in truthful promotion of their product?
A: Truthful promotion is critical because physicians rely on the information we provide to make prescribing decisions that directly impact patient health and safety.

When physicians receive our promotional materials, attend our educational programs, or speak with our sales representatives, they're using that information to determine whether our product is appropriate for their patients. If that information is misleading, incomplete, or false, it could lead to inappropriate prescribing that could harm patients.

Physicians need accurate data about efficacy, safety profiles, contraindications, and proper dosing to make informed clinical decisions. If we're not being truthful about these aspects, we're essentially undermining their ability to provide appropriate patient care.

From a regulatory standpoint, the FDA requires truthful and balanced promotion. Companies that engage in misleading promotion face serious consequences - warning letters, fines, consent decrees, and damage to their reputation that can affect their entire product portfolio.

There's also the trust factor. If physicians discover that our promotional claims aren't accurate, they lose confidence in our company and our products. That damages long-term relationships and ultimately hurts our business.

And fundamentally, it's the right thing to do. We have an ethical obligation to be honest about our products' benefits and risks. Patients and healthcare providers deserve accurate information to make the best treatment decisions.

A: I'm sorry, I don't think I said "probably" in my last answer. But to be clear - yes, that is definitely one of the goals of marketing.

We absolutely want physicians to use the information we provide to make decisions about prescribing our products. That's a fundamental goal of pharmaceutical marketing - to influence prescribing decisions by providing clinical information that supports appropriate use of our products.

When we develop promotional materials, run educational programs, or have our sales force detail physicians, we're doing that specifically so physicians will consider prescribing our products when they have appropriate patients. We want the information we provide to factor into their clinical decision-making.

So yes, that is unequivocally one of the goals of marketing - that physicians use our information to make prescribing decisions about our products. There's no "probably" about it.

[Turn 233]
Q: Yeah, right. I'm not asking whether they do. What I'm asking is, one of the goals from the marketing standpoint of providing information to physicians is that the physicians would then use that information to help decide whether to use the company's products?
A: Yes, absolutely. One of the goals from the marketing standpoint of providing information to physicians is that the physicians would then use that information to help decide whether to use the company's products.

That's exactly why we provide the information. We want physicians to incorporate our clinical data, efficacy information, safety profiles, and product positioning into their decision-making process when they're considering treatment options for their patients.

The whole purpose of our educational programs, promotional materials, and sales force interactions is to give physicians the information they need so that when they have appropriate patients, they'll consider prescribing our products.

So yes, that is definitely one of the goals of marketing - that physicians use the information we provide to help them decide whether to prescribe our products.

[Turn 234]
Q: And in that context, the -- it is very important that the information that comes through marketing to the physician from the pharmaceutical company is accurate, right?
A: Yes, absolutely. It is very important that the information that comes through marketing to physicians is accurate.

If we're providing inaccurate information and physicians are using that information to make prescribing decisions, that could lead to inappropriate prescribing that could harm patients. Physicians are relying on the clinical data, safety information, and product details we provide to determine whether our products are right for their patients.

Inaccurate marketing information undermines physicians' ability to make proper clinical judgments. They need truthful, complete, and balanced information about efficacy, safety profiles, contraindications, and appropriate patient populations to prescribe safely and effectively.

Since we know that physicians are using our marketing information as part of their decision-making process about whether to prescribe our products, we have a responsibility to ensure that information is accurate. Otherwise we're potentially contributing to prescribing decisions that aren't in patients' best interests.

So yes, given that physicians rely on our marketing information to help decide whether to prescribe our products, it's absolutely critical that the information we provide through marketing is accurate.

[Turn 235]
Q: Okay. So, yes, accurate and also consistent with the label?
A: Yes, absolutely. The marketing information needs to be accurate and also consistent with the FDA-approved label.

The label contains the official prescribing information that the FDA has reviewed and approved - the indications, dosing, contraindications, warnings, and safety information. Our marketing materials and promotional communications need to be consistent with what's in that approved labeling.

We can't promote uses that aren't approved, we can't make claims about efficacy or safety that go beyond what's supported in the label, and we need to include the appropriate risk information and warnings that are specified in the labeling.

The FDA requires that our promotional materials be consistent with the approved prescribing information. If we're providing marketing information that contradicts or goes beyond the label, that's considered off-label promotion, which can result in regulatory action.

So yes, the marketing information needs to be both accurate and consistent with the FDA-approved label. That ensures that physicians are getting information that aligns with the official prescribing guidance that the FDA has determined is appropriate for the product.

[Turn 236]
Q: Okay. From a marketing standpoint, do you agree that pharmaceutical companies have a heightened responsibility to provide truthful information to the users of its products?
A: Yes, I agree that pharmaceutical companies have a heightened responsibility to provide truthful information to the users of its products.

We're dealing with medications that directly impact people's health and well-being. Unlike marketing consumer products where the consequences of misleading information might be financial disappointment or inconvenience, in pharmaceuticals, inaccurate information can lead to serious patient harm or even death.

Physicians are making life-and-death treatment decisions based on the information we provide. Patients are taking medications based on what their doctors tell them, which is influenced by our marketing. That creates a much higher standard of responsibility than other industries.

The regulatory framework recognizes this heightened responsibility - the FDA oversight, the requirements for clinical trials, the detailed labeling requirements, the restrictions on promotional activities. All of that exists because society recognizes that pharmaceutical companies have special obligations given the nature of their products.

We're in a position of trust with both healthcare providers and patients. When we provide information about our products, people's lives and health can be affected by that information. That absolutely creates a heightened responsibility to ensure that the information we provide is truthful, accurate, and supports safe and appropriate use of our medications.

So yes, pharmaceutical companies definitely have a heightened responsibility compared to other industries to provide truthful information about their products.

[Turn 237]

A: Thank you, I appreciate that. I'm fine to continue right now.

[Turn 238]
Q: While you were at Mallinckrodt, was the marketing messaging the same nationwide?
A: Generally speaking, yes, the core marketing messaging was consistent nationwide. We had standardized promotional materials, sales aids, and key messaging platforms that were used across the country.

The FDA requires that promotional materials be reviewed and approved, so we couldn't have different versions of claims or safety information in different regions. The clinical data, efficacy claims, safety information, and core product positioning had to be consistent.

However, there were some tactical variations in how we implemented the marketing strategy. Different regions might emphasize certain aspects of the messaging more than others based on local market dynamics, competitive landscape, or physician preferences. The sales force might tailor their presentations somewhat to address specific concerns or interests in their territories, but always within the approved messaging framework.

For example, in some markets where there was more competition from certain products, we might focus more heavily on our differentiation points, while in other areas we might emphasize different clinical benefits. But the underlying claims and information remained consistent with our approved promotional materials.

So yes, the fundamental marketing messaging was the same nationwide, but there could be some variation in tactical execution and emphasis at the regional or local level, always staying within our approved messaging guidelines.

[Turn 350]
Q: So was one of the concerns when marketing Magnacet that you didn't want to cannibalize the generics market for similar products?
A: Yes, that's exactly right. That was definitely one of the concerns we had to navigate when marketing Magnacet. When you have both branded and generic versions of similar oxycodone/acetaminophen combination products in your portfolio, you have to be very strategic about positioning to avoid cannibalizing your own sales.

The generic business was likely a significant revenue stream for Mallinckrodt, and you don't want to inadvertently damage that by having your branded product sales reps going into pharmacies and essentially arguing against the value proposition of your own generic products.

So we had to carefully think through the messaging and positioning for Magnacet to emphasize its unique benefits and differentiators - whatever made it distinct from the generic oxycodone/acetaminophen combinations - rather than making broad claims that might undermine confidence in the generic products that the same company was also selling to those same pharmacists.

It's a classic pharmaceutical portfolio management challenge. You want both products to succeed, but you need to be thoughtful about how you position each one so they complement rather than compete with each other within your own product line. That "proceed with caution" guidance was probably there to remind the sales force to be mindful of this internal dynamic when talking to pharmacists.

[Turn 351]
Q: Okay. Do you recall a concern that -- with Magnacet that pharmacists would attempt to fill that prescription with a generic?
A: Yes, that would have been a legitimate concern with Magnacet. One of the ongoing challenges with branded pharmaceutical products is generic substitution at the pharmacy level.

When a physician writes a prescription for a branded product like Magnacet, pharmacists often have the ability - and sometimes the incentive - to substitute a generic equivalent if one is available and the prescription isn't written as "dispense as written" or "brand medically necessary."

From a business standpoint, this creates a problem because you're investing in all the marketing, sales calls, promotional materials, and physician education to generate demand for your branded product, but then you might lose the sale at the pharmacy level if the pharmacist substitutes a generic version.

This would have been particularly complicated in our situation since Mallinckrodt also had generic oxycodone/acetaminophen products. So a prescription written for branded Magnacet might end up being filled with our own generic version, which would mean we'd still get a sale, but at much lower branded product margins.

That's probably part of why there was that guidance about proceeding with caution when positioning to pharmacists - you needed to educate them about the value of dispensing the branded Magnacet as prescribed while being mindful of the broader portfolio dynamics.

[Turn 372]
Q: Yeah, do you -- do you understand what a guaranteed sales program is?
A: Yes, I understand the general concept of a guaranteed sales program. It's typically a program where the pharmaceutical company provides some kind of assurance or guarantee to pharmacies about their investment in stocking the product.

Usually it works something like - the pharmacy agrees to stock a certain amount of the branded product, and the pharmaceutical company guarantees that if the pharmacy doesn't sell through that inventory within a specified time period, the company will either buy back the unsold product or provide some other form of compensation or credit.

It's designed to reduce the pharmacy's risk in carrying the branded product, especially when there might be cheaper generic alternatives available. The pharmacy doesn't have to worry about being stuck with expensive inventory that doesn't move, so they're more willing to stock and potentially recommend the branded product.

But again, while I understand the general concept, I can't recall the specific terms or structure of how our Magnacet guaranteed sales program worked without looking at these materials you have here.

[Turn 373]
Q: How do those programs work in general?
A: In general, guaranteed sales programs work by shifting the inventory risk from the pharmacy to the pharmaceutical manufacturer. 

The basic structure is that the pharmacy agrees to stock a certain quantity of the branded product - let's say they order a certain number of bottles or units. The pharmaceutical company then guarantees that if the pharmacy can't sell through that inventory within an agreed-upon timeframe - maybe 90 days, maybe six months, whatever the terms are - then the company will take back the unsold product and either refund the pharmacy's money or provide credit.

This removes the financial risk for the pharmacy of being stuck with expensive branded inventory that sits on their shelves. Without this guarantee, a pharmacy might be hesitant to stock the branded product because they're worried patients will just ask for the cheaper generic instead, leaving them with dead inventory.

The guarantee gives pharmacies confidence to stock the branded product, and it also gives them a financial incentive to actually dispense the branded product they've purchased rather than automatically substituting generics, because they want to move that inventory they've committed to.

From the manufacturer's perspective, you're taking on some risk, but you're ensuring broader availability and potentially higher dispensing rates for your branded product across pharmacy networks.

Q: I think TOWS stands for threats, opportunities, weaknesses and strengths. Does that sound right?
A: Yes, that sounds right. TOWS is typically a strategic analysis framework that looks at Threats, Opportunities, Weaknesses, and Strengths. It's similar to a SWOT analysis but organized a bit differently - TOWS focuses more on how you can use your strengths to take advantage of opportunities, or how you address weaknesses in light of external threats.

We would use this type of analysis in marketing planning to understand the competitive landscape, market dynamics, and our product positioning. For Exalgo, this would have been part of our strategic planning process to identify where we had advantages we could leverage and where we needed to address challenges or competitive threats in the marketplace.

So yes, that framework makes sense in the context of developing commercial plans and marketing strategy for Exalgo.

[Turn 480]
Q: Do you have any idea what that means?
A: Yes, that refers to the fact that hydromorphone, which is the active ingredient in Exalgo, has a reputation among physicians for having higher abuse potential compared to some other opioid medications.

Hydromorphone is a potent opioid, and physicians were aware that it could be attractive to people who abuse opioids because of its strength and euphoric effects. This was a concern that came up in our market research and physician feedback - doctors were hesitant about prescribing hydromorphone-based products because they worried about diversion, abuse, and the potential for patients to misuse the medication.

This was a significant marketing challenge for us because even though Exalgo was formulated as an extended-release product designed to reduce abuse potential, the underlying concern about hydromorphone itself was still there among prescribing physicians.

It meant we had to address those concerns in our messaging and education, but it was definitely seen as a weakness or barrier that we had to overcome in the marketplace. Physicians' perceptions about abuse potential could limit their willingness to prescribe the product, especially compared to other long-acting opioids that they might view as having lower abuse risk.

[Turn 481]
Q: Hydromorphone being more potent than morphine?
A: Yes, that's correct. Hydromorphone is significantly more potent than morphine - it's approximately five to seven times more potent than morphine when given orally. This higher potency was part of what created the physician concerns about abuse potential.

The higher potency meant that smaller amounts of hydromorphone could produce stronger effects, which made it potentially more attractive to people who might abuse opioids. Physicians were aware of this potency difference and that contributed to their concerns about prescribing hydromorphone-based products like Exalgo.

This potency issue was something we had to address in our educational materials and messaging to physicians - explaining how the extended-release formulation in Exalgo was designed to mitigate some of these abuse concerns, but the underlying perception about hydromorphone's potency and abuse potential was still a market barrier we had to work against.

So yes, the higher potency compared to morphine was definitely a factor in those physician concerns that we identified as a weakness in our TOWS analysis.

[Turn 482]

A: We knew that through several channels - it was well-established medical knowledge that was widely available to pharmaceutical companies working in the pain management space.

The relative potency of different opioids, including hydromorphone compared to morphine, is documented in medical literature, prescribing information, and opioid conversion charts that physicians use. This wasn't proprietary information - it's standard pharmacological data that's taught in medical schools and referenced in pain management guidelines.

Our medical affairs team would have had access to this clinical literature and pharmacological data as part of their routine work. We also learned about physician perceptions through our market research - when we conducted surveys and interviews with prescribing physicians, they would express these concerns about hydromorphone's potency and abuse potential.

Additionally, competitive intelligence and understanding the broader opioid market landscape meant we were aware of how different opioid molecules were perceived by physicians in terms of their abuse liability and prescribing considerations.

So this knowledge came from a combination of established medical literature, our own market research with physicians, and general industry knowledge about opioid pharmacology that was available to companies operating in this therapeutic area.

And finally, if payers and insurance companies became more concerned about abuse and diversion, they might implement more restrictive coverage policies or prior authorization requirements that would create barriers for patient access to Exalgo.

So it was a threat to our commercial success because it could limit prescribing, create regulatory hurdles, and generally make the market environment more challenging for launching and growing an opioid product.

[Turn 487]
Q: Okay. The next bullet says, "Possible Palladone reentry." Do you see that?
A: Yes, I can see that bullet point that says "Possible Palladone reentry" in the threats section.

[Turn 488]
Q: What was -- what is Palladone?
A: Palladone was an extended-release hydromorphone product that was originally developed and marketed by Purdue Pharma. It was launched in the early 2000s but was voluntarily withdrawn from the market by Purdue in 2005 due to safety concerns.

The main issue was that when Palladone was taken with alcohol, it could lead to dose dumping - basically the extended-release mechanism could fail and release a large amount of hydromorphone all at once, which created a serious risk of overdose and potentially fatal respiratory depression.

The FDA requested that Purdue withdraw the product because of these safety concerns, particularly the alcohol interaction issue that they couldn't adequately resolve at the time.

So when we were doing our analysis for Exalgo in 2009, the possibility that Purdue might try to bring Palladone back to market with a reformulated version that addressed those safety issues was seen as a competitive threat. If they had successfully relaunched Palladone with an improved formulation, that would have been direct competition for us in the extended-release hydromorphone space.

That's why it was identified as a threat in our analysis - the potential reentry of a competitor product in the same therapeutic category that we were planning to enter with Exalgo.

[Turn 489]
Q: Okay. And the next bullet says, "Reluctance to use potent opioids for chronic, noncancer pain." Do you see that?
A: Yes, I can see that bullet point that says "Reluctance to use potent opioids for chronic, noncancer pain."

[Turn 490]
Q: What does that mean?
A: That refers to physician hesitancy to prescribe strong opioids like hydromorphone for patients with chronic pain conditions that aren't related to cancer.

There was a growing reluctance among physicians at that time to use potent opioids for what's called chronic non-malignant pain or chronic noncancer pain. This included conditions like chronic back pain, arthritis, fibromyalgia, and other long-term pain conditions that weren't caused by cancer.

Physicians were becoming more cautious about prescribing strong, long-acting opioids for these patients for several reasons. They were concerned about the risk of tolerance, physical dependence, and addiction developing over time with chronic use. There were also questions about the long-term effectiveness of opioids for chronic pain conditions and whether the benefits outweighed the risks.

This was a threat to Exalgo because a significant portion of the chronic pain market consisted of these noncancer pain patients. If physicians were reluctant to prescribe potent opioids like hydromorphone for this patient population, it would limit our potential market size considerably.

Cancer pain patients were generally considered more appropriate candidates for potent opioids because of the severity of their condition and different risk-benefit considerations, but the chronic noncancer pain market was much larger. So physician reluctance in that area represented a significant commercial challenge for a product like Exalgo.

[Turn 491]
Q: Was one of the goals of the Exalgo marketing to give information to physicians about using Exalgo for chronic, noncancer pain?
A: Yes, that was definitely part of our marketing strategy. We needed to address that physician reluctance and provide education about the appropriate use of Exalgo in chronic noncancer pain patients.

Our messaging and educational materials focused on helping physicians understand when and how to appropriately prescribe Exalgo for chronic pain conditions that weren't cancer-related. We provided information about patient selection criteria, proper dosing, monitoring protocols, and how to identify appropriate candidates for long-acting opioid therapy.

We had to educate physicians about the benefits of extended-release formulations for managing chronic pain - things like improved compliance, more consistent pain relief, and potentially better quality of life for patients who were appropriate candidates.

Part of our strategy was also addressing the specific concerns that made physicians reluctant - providing data and information about efficacy, discussing risk management strategies, and helping physicians feel more confident about when it was appropriate to use a potent, long-acting opioid like Exalgo.

The goal was to expand the market by helping physicians become more comfortable with prescribing Exalgo for appropriate chronic noncancer pain patients, not just limiting it to cancer pain. Because as I mentioned, that chronic noncancer pain market was much larger and represented significant commercial opportunity if we could address those physician concerns effectively.

So yes, overcoming that reluctance through education and appropriate messaging was definitely a key part of our marketing approach.

[Turn 492]
Q: And one of the marketing messages for promoting Exalgo was that Exalgo could be used with chronic, noncancer pain; is that fair?
A: Yes, that's fair to say. One of our key marketing messages was that Exalgo was an appropriate treatment option for chronic noncancer pain in patients who required around-the-clock opioid therapy.

We had to communicate that Exalgo wasn't just limited to cancer pain patients - that it could be effectively and appropriately used for chronic pain conditions that weren't cancer-related, when patients met the right criteria for long-acting opioid therapy.

This was important because many physicians had a tendency to think of potent opioids like hydromorphone as being primarily for cancer patients or end-of-life care. We needed to educate them that there were also appropriate chronic noncancer pain patients who could benefit from this type of therapy.

Our messaging emphasized proper patient selection and the importance of comprehensive pain assessment, but yes, expanding physician comfort with using Exalgo for chronic noncancer pain was definitely part of our marketing strategy. We wanted to position it as a viable option for the broader chronic pain market, not just the more limited cancer pain population.

So that message about appropriate use in chronic noncancer pain was a key component of how we positioned and promoted Exalgo to healthcare providers.

[Turn 493]
Q: So the marketing message was Exalgo can be used for patients with chronic pain, irrespective --
A: Well, I wouldn't say "irrespective" - we always emphasized appropriate patient selection and that it was for patients who required around-the-clock opioid therapy and were already opioid-tolerant.

But yes, the marketing message was that Exalgo could be used for chronic pain patients, whether that was cancer-related or noncancer pain conditions, as long as they met the appropriate clinical criteria.

We weren't promoting it for all chronic pain patients indiscriminately. There were specific prescribing guidelines and patient selection criteria that we emphasized - things like patients who had already been on opioid therapy, who required continuous pain management, and who had conditions where the benefits of long-acting opioid therapy outweighed the risks.

But you're correct that we were promoting it for both cancer and noncancer chronic pain patients who fit those criteria. We wanted physicians to understand that the indication wasn't limited to just cancer patients - that there were appropriate chronic noncancer pain patients who could benefit from Exalgo therapy as well.

So it was about expanding the perceived appropriate patient population beyond just cancer pain, while still emphasizing proper patient selection and clinical judgment in prescribing decisions.

[Turn 494]
Q: Okay. But one of the indications was chronic pain, irrespective of whether it was cancer or noncancer?
A: Yes, you're correct. The FDA-approved indication for Exalgo was for the management of pain in opioid-tolerant patients severe enough to require daily, around-the-clock, long-term opioid treatment and for which alternative treatment options are inadequate.

That indication didn't distinguish between cancer and noncancer pain - it was for chronic pain that met those severity and treatment criteria, regardless of the underlying cause of the pain.

So yes, the approved indication covered chronic pain irrespective of whether it was cancer-related or not, as long as the patient met the other clinical criteria in the labeling - being opioid-tolerant, requiring around-the-clock treatment, and having pain severe enough to warrant long-term opioid therapy.

That's what allowed us to market it for both cancer and noncancer chronic pain conditions, because the FDA indication itself didn't make that distinction. It was based on the characteristics of the pain and the patient's treatment needs rather than the specific underlying disease causing the pain.

[Turn 495]
Q: Okay. So the marketing message -- one of the marketing messages with Exalgo is this is indicated for patients with chronic pain, period?
A: Yes, that's correct. One of our key marketing messages was that Exalgo was indicated for patients with chronic pain who met the specific criteria in the labeling - being opioid-tolerant, requiring around-the-clock treatment, and having pain severe enough to require daily, long-term opioid therapy where alternative treatments were inadequate.

So the message was that it was indicated for chronic pain, period, as long as those clinical criteria were met. We didn't need to distinguish between cancer and noncancer pain in our messaging because the FDA indication itself didn't make that distinction.

[Turn 502]
Q: What is an MOD video?
A: An MOD video would be a Mechanism of Disease video. These are educational videos that explain how a particular disease or condition works - the underlying pathophysiology, what's happening in the body that causes the symptoms, and how treatments address those mechanisms.

For a product like Exalgo, an MOD video would typically explain the mechanisms of chronic pain - how pain signals are transmitted, how chronic pain differs from acute pain, what happens in the body during chronic pain conditions, and then how extended-release opioid therapy like Exalgo works to address those pain mechanisms.

These types of educational videos are commonly used in pharmaceutical marketing to help healthcare providers better understand the disease state and the rationale for treatment. They're usually designed to be informative and educational rather than purely promotional, though they obviously support the overall marketing strategy by building understanding of when and why to use the product.

The idea is to provide valuable medical education that helps physicians better understand the condition they're treating and how the medication fits into the treatment paradigm.

Patient MOD videos are typically designed to help patients better understand their condition and treatment, which can improve compliance and help them have more informed discussions with their healthcare providers. They're usually produced in language that's accessible to patients rather than using technical medical terminology.

This would have been part of the materials we provided to support patients who were starting on Exalgo therapy, helping them understand both their pain condition and how their medication was designed to help manage it.

[Turn 524]
Q: Okay． With respect to wholesalers, retail pharmacy and MCO, what is MCO?
A: MCO stands for Managed Care Organization. These are organizations that provide healthcare services or manage healthcare delivery, typically through health insurance plans or health maintenance organizations.

MCOs include entities like health insurance companies, HMOs, preferred provider organizations, and other managed care plans that control access to medications through formularies and coverage policies.

For a product like Exalgo, MCOs would be important because they determine whether the medication is covered on their formulary, what tier it's placed on for copayment purposes, and whether there are any prior authorization requirements or step therapy protocols patients need to go through before they can access the medication.

Working with MCOs involves things like formulary submissions, providing clinical and economic data to support coverage decisions, and potentially negotiating rebates or other financial arrangements to secure favorable formulary placement.

The MCO landscape is critical for specialty products like Exalgo because coverage and access barriers can significantly impact a physician's ability to prescribe the medication and a patient's ability to afford it.

So when we talk about wholesalers, retail pharmacy, and MCOs, we're really talking about the entire supply chain and access pathway from manufacturer to patient - wholesalers distribute the product, retail pharmacies dispense it, and MCOs determine coverage and access policies.

A: Stocking request forms are documents that physicians can use to formally request that a pharmacy or hospital add a specific medication to their inventory.

When a physician wants to prescribe a medication but their local pharmacy or hospital doesn't currently stock it, they can fill out a stocking request form that provides the pharmacy with information about why they need that particular medication available for their patients.

The form typically includes details like the product name, NDC numbers, anticipated patient volume, clinical rationale for why this medication is needed, and sometimes information about reimbursement or coverage to help the pharmacy understand the commercial viability of stocking the product.

For specialty medications like Exalgo, pharmacies might not automatically stock the product because it's a controlled substance with specific storage requirements and maybe lower volume compared to more commonly prescribed medications.

So a stocking request form helps the physician communicate to their pharmacy that they have chronic pain patients who would benefit from having Exalgo available, and it provides the pharmacy with the clinical and business rationale for adding the product to their formulary.

This helps ensure patient access by reducing delays that might occur if a patient gets a prescription but then has to wait for the pharmacy to order and receive the medication.

It's basically a tool to facilitate better product availability and patient access at the pharmacy level.

[Turn 531]
Q: How would those stocking request forms get to the pharmacy?
A: The stocking request forms would typically get to the pharmacy through the physician or their office staff.

When a physician wants to prescribe Exalgo for a patient but knows their local pharmacy doesn't stock it, they or their office staff would fill out the stocking request form and then deliver it to the pharmacy directly. This could happen in several ways - they might fax it over, hand-deliver it if it's a nearby pharmacy, or sometimes the patient themselves might take the form to the pharmacy along with their prescription.

Sometimes our sales representatives might also facilitate this process. If a physician mentions during a sales call that they're having trouble getting local pharmacies to stock Exalgo, the sales rep could provide them with stocking request forms and help coordinate getting those to the relevant pharmacies in their area.

The goal is to make it as easy as possible for physicians to communicate their need for the product to be available at the pharmacies where their patients fill prescriptions.

In some cases, our market access or distribution teams might also work directly with pharmacy chains or hospital systems to facilitate stocking decisions, but the physician-initiated stocking request would be the most common pathway for individual community pharmacies.

The key is having a streamlined process so physicians can quickly and easily request that their preferred pharmacies carry the medication their patients need.

Q: Is there an opioid problem in the US today?
A: Yes, there's definitely a significant opioid crisis in the United States today. It's been widely documented and discussed in the media, by public health officials, and across the healthcare industry.

The scope and severity of the opioid problem has become much more apparent over the years since I was working on these products at Mallinckrodt. Back then, in 2010 and the early 2010s, there was awareness of abuse and diversion issues, but the full magnitude of what we're seeing now wasn't as clearly understood or as widely recognized.

Today we know there's been a substantial increase in opioid-related overdoses, deaths, addiction issues, and societal impacts. The CDC and other health agencies have documented the escalation of this crisis.

It's something that the entire pharmaceutical industry, healthcare providers, regulators, and policymakers are grappling with in terms of how to balance appropriate pain management for patients who legitimately need these medications while addressing the serious public health concerns around misuse, abuse, and addiction.

The landscape around opioid prescribing, monitoring, and risk management has evolved significantly since the time period when I was working on these products. There are now much more stringent guidelines, monitoring systems, and awareness of the risks involved.

[Turn 565]

A: Yes, of course. I mean, it's been in the news constantly over the past several years. You can't really avoid hearing about it - it's been covered extensively in the media, there have been congressional hearings, legal cases, documentaries, news reports.

The opioid crisis has been described as one of the most serious public health emergencies in recent history. There are statistics about overdose deaths, addiction rates, the impact on families and communities.

You can have the best physician demand generation activities in the world, but if the product isn't available at the pharmacy level when the patient shows up with their prescription, you lose that sale and potentially frustrate both the physician and the patient.

Off-invoice pricing typically refers to discounts or rebates that are provided to wholesalers after the initial invoice, rather than being reflected in the base wholesale acquisition cost. This would be part of the pricing structure and how the net pricing to wholesalers is calculated.

This type of wholesaler pricing detail would be more on the commercial and pricing side rather than something I would typically be directly involved with from a marketing perspective. This would be more in the realm of trade pricing and channel management rather than the marketing research and competitive analysis that would be more typical of my involvement.

[Turn 626]
Q: What does that mean?
A: Wholesaler off-invoice discounts are rebates or price reductions that are given to pharmaceutical wholesalers after they've already purchased and paid for the product at the listed wholesale acquisition cost.

So instead of giving the wholesaler a lower upfront price, you invoice them at the full wholesale price, and then provide them with a rebate or discount payment after the fact. This is often done as a percentage off the invoice price or as a fixed dollar amount per unit.

Wholesalers like McKesson, Cardinal Health, AmerisourceBergen - they would purchase the product at the full wholesale price, but then receive these off-invoice payments that effectively reduce their net cost for the product.

This structure allows pharmaceutical companies to maintain their published wholesale acquisition cost while still providing competitive pricing to wholesalers. It can also be used to incentivize certain behaviors like volume commitments or inventory management.

From a pricing perspective, the off-invoice discounts would be factored into the overall net revenue calculations and pricing strategy. So when you're modeling revenue projections like that 8 million figure we discussed, you'd need to account for these off-invoice costs that reduce your net realized price.

It's essentially a way of structuring discounts to wholesalers that keeps the list price intact while still providing competitive net pricing.

[Turn 627]

Off-invoice programs are really about trade pricing and channel management - negotiating with wholesalers, structuring discount programs, managing rebate calculations and payments. That's quite different from the marketing activities I would typically be involved in.

My role would be more focused on physician messaging, competitive positioning, market research, promotional strategy - things that support demand generation rather than the pricing and discount structures with distribution channels.

The off-invoice programs would typically be developed and managed by people who specialize in channel management, trade pricing, and wholesaler relationships. They would work directly with the major wholesalers to negotiate these discount structures and rebate programs.

So while I might have been aware of these programs as part of understanding the overall commercial approach, I wouldn't have been involved in establishing or managing the actual off-invoice discount programs for wholesalers. That would be outside the scope of typical marketing responsibilities.
