This would be a key insight for developing market access strategies and working with payers to improve formulary positioning and reduce patient cost burden.

[Turn 686]
Q: What does that mean?
A: It means that the main reasons physicians weren't prescribing Xartemis XR, or patients weren't getting it filled, were related to cost and insurance coverage issues.

So a doctor might want to prescribe Xartemis XR for a patient, but if the patient's insurance doesn't cover it well or requires a high copay - maybe fifty or a hundred dollars instead of ten or twenty dollars for a generic - then either the physician won't prescribe it in the first place, or the patient won't fill the prescription when they get to the pharmacy.

"Managed care coverage" refers to whether insurance plans, HMOs, and other payers have the product on their formularies and what tier it's placed on. If it's not covered or on a high-cost tier, that creates a barrier.

These are access issues rather than clinical issues - it's not that doctors don't think the product works or is appropriate, it's that the financial barriers are preventing patients from actually getting the medication. This would drive strategies around working with insurance companies, developing patient assistance programs, or other approaches to reduce the cost burden.

[Turn 687]
Q: Did you play a role in marketing Xartemis to managed care plans?
A: I may have had some involvement in supporting managed care activities for Xartemis XR, but that wouldn't have been my primary responsibility.

Managed care marketing is typically handled by specialized payer relations or market access teams who have expertise in health economics, outcomes research, and payer contracting. They're the ones who would develop the value propositions for P&T committees, negotiate formulary placement, and manage the ongoing payer relationships.

My role would have been more on the brand strategy side - understanding how payer coverage issues impact our overall marketing approach, providing input on messaging that might be relevant for payer discussions, and working with the market access team to ensure our brand positioning aligns with what payers are looking for.

But the day-to-day work of actually marketing to managed care plans - preparing the clinical and economic dossiers, presenting to pharmacy and therapeutics committees, negotiating rebate contracts - that would be handled by the specialized market access function rather than product management.

So while I would be aware of these coverage challenges as a key barrier to prescribing, the actual managed care marketing activities would typically be someone else's primary responsibility.

[Turn 688]
Q: Okay. Next bullet is, "Coverage is consistent with other recently launched brands; however, market is ubiquitously generic." Do you see that?
A: Yes, I see that bullet point - "Coverage is consistent with other recently launched brands; however, market is ubiquitously generic."

That's describing a challenging market dynamic for Xartemis XR. The coverage part means that from a formulary perspective, Xartemis XR was getting similar treatment to other new branded products - so the payer access wasn't necessarily worse than what you'd expect for a newly launched brand.

But the "market is ubiquitously generic" part highlights the real challenge - the pain medication market was dominated by generic alternatives. So even if you have decent formulary coverage, you're still competing against much cheaper generic opioid combinations that physicians and patients are familiar with.

This creates a difficult value proposition - you need to demonstrate enough clinical or practical advantage to justify the higher cost compared to generic options that are widely available and much less expensive. It's not just about getting on formulary, it's about convincing prescribers and patients that the benefits of the branded product are worth the additional cost when generics are so prevalent in the market.

[Turn 689]
Q: What does that mean?
A: It means that while Xartemis XR was getting reasonable insurance coverage compared to other new branded drugs, the pain medication market was completely dominated by cheap generic alternatives.

So essentially, physicians had plenty of inexpensive generic opioid options available - things like generic Percocet, generic Vicodin, other generic pain medications that cost patients maybe five or ten dollars. These generics were widely available and physicians were comfortable prescribing them.

When you're trying to launch a new branded product like Xartemis XR into that environment, you're asking physicians to choose your product - which might cost patients fifty or a hundred dollars even with insurance - over these tried-and-true generic options that cost almost nothing.

Even if insurance covers your product reasonably well, there's still usually a significant cost difference between a branded drug and a generic. And when the market is "ubiquitously generic" - meaning generics are everywhere and dominating prescribing patterns - it's very difficult to get physicians to switch to your more expensive option.

You have to make a compelling case for why your product is worth the extra cost when effective, cheaper alternatives are readily available.

[Turn 690]
Q: That was a challenge to marketing Xartemis?
A: Absolutely, yes. That was a major marketing challenge for Xartemis XR.

When you're launching into a market that's dominated by generics, you're fighting an uphill battle on multiple fronts. Physicians are comfortable with the generic options they've been using, patients prefer the lower costs, and you have to justify why someone should pay significantly more for your branded product.

From a marketing perspective, this means you need to develop very compelling value propositions - whether that's around the abuse-deterrent properties, the specific formulation benefits, convenience factors, or other clinical advantages that justify the price premium.

You also have to work much harder on education because physicians need to understand not just what your product does, but why it's worth choosing over the cheaper alternatives they're already familiar with.

It affects everything from your messaging strategy to your target physician selection to how you structure your sales conversations. Instead of just explaining product benefits, you're constantly having to overcome the cost objection and demonstrate superior value compared to established generic competitors.

So yes, that ubiquitous generic competition definitely made marketing Xartemis XR much more challenging than if we were entering a market without well-established, inexpensive alternatives.

[Turn 691]
Q: And Mallinckrodt put in place a co-pay discount program for Xartemis at some point?
A: Yes, I believe we did implement a co-pay discount program for Xartemis XR. That's a pretty standard approach when you're dealing with cost barriers like what we just discussed - when patient out-of-pocket costs are identified as a top limitation to prescribing.

Co-pay programs help bridge that gap between what insurance covers and what patients can afford to pay. If a patient's copay is normally fifty or a hundred dollars, a co-pay card might reduce that to ten or twenty dollars, making it much more competitive with generic alternatives.

Given that patient out-of-pocket costs were identified as one of the main barriers to Xartemis XR prescribing, implementing a co-pay discount program would make sense as part of the market access strategy to remove that cost barrier and make the product more accessible to patients.

These programs are pretty common for branded pharmaceutical products when you're competing against generics or when high patient costs are limiting uptake.
